                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20materials">carbon materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Microcrystalline+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_microcrystalline%20structure">microcrystalline structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Descriptor&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_descriptor">descriptor</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Pitch&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_pitch">pitch</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec0" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been widely considered as a promising complement to the costly lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the current energy storage market due to sodium’s abundant reserve in the earth’s crust, its high safety, and a similar rocking-chair mechanism with LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> SIBs share similar polyanion-type cathode materials and ester/ether-based electrolytes with LIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> whereas the anode materials in SIBs are apparently distinct from the graphite anodes adopted by LIBs due to the much larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> than Li<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> Developing a high-performance anode material thus matters fundamentally for the design of high-performance SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 8 to 12">(8−12)</a> Among the anode materials, compared with alloying materials and conversion materials that suffer from volume expansion and powder pulverization, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> carbon-based materials with good structural stability and high electric conductivity are widely accepted as one of the most promising anode materials for commercialization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> A variety of carbon-based materials have been developed as anode materials for SIBs, e.g., hard carbon, soft carbon, and porous carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> For example, a hard carbon anode material was prepared with sucrose as the carbon source, which delivers a capacity of 220 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> A mesoporous soft carbon (MSC) derived from the mesophase pitch shows a capacity of 260 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. These relatively low specific capacities suffer from small interlayer spacing, long ion diffusion distance, or high graphitization degree. Introducing a heteroatom into the carbon matrix was reported to be capable of extending the interlayer spacing and suppressing graphitization, for example, N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> and S-doped carbon <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> achieved relatively high capacities of 321 and 330 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the porous structures also create disordered regions with large interlayer distances to facilitate the insertion/extraction of sodium ions. For instance, a mesoporous soft carbon prepared from pitch using nano-CaCO<sub>3</sub> as the template has a layer spacing of 0.374 nm and thus achieves a specific capacity of 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Compared with the amorphous carbon, a porous three-dimensional amorphous carbon (3DAC) with a more disordered structure was prepared by the NaCl template method and shows a layer spacing of 0.408 nm and a specific capacity of 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Hence, regulating the microcrystalline structure to realize the short-range ordered yet long-range disordered microstructure plays a significant role in improving the sodium storage performance of carbon anode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The cost factor is widely discussed as one major advantage of SIBs over LIBs in future large-scale applications, which calls for the low-cost preparation of anodic carbon materials. Currently, many carbon anodes stem from organic polymers (e.g., phenolic resin, epoxy resin), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28 to 31">(28−31)</a> and biomasses (e.g., starch, sucrose, glucose). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32−34)</a> The usage of these precursors increases the cost of carbon materials. On the one hand, the low atomic ratio of C atoms in organic polymers and biomasses and the carbon corrosion caused by the intrinsically abundant H and O atoms usually lead to a low yield rate (&lt;20%) of carbon materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> On the other hand, it requires a high energy consumption to overcome the energy barriers for the thermal-driven rearrangement of C atoms during the carbonization of organic polymers and biomasses. In contrast, precursors featured by polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons such as pitch demonstrate great potentials in preparing carbon materials for SIBs in a low-cost way. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> The rich aromatic fragments have a high proportion of C atoms, resulting in a high yield rate (&gt;40%) of carbon materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> which in association with the low cost and easy acquisition from coal/petroleum chemical industries lay the foundation for the industrialization application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> However, the strong π–π interaction during the carbonization process makes it easy for polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons to form parallelly stacked carbon sheets, showing high graphitization with low interlayer spacing and impeding the mass transfer and storage of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Thus, regulating the microcrystalline structure and retarding graphitization are important to prepare polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon carbon (PAHC) for promoting sodium-ion storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">It was reported that the polymerization and carbonization of aromatic fragments can be regulated through introducing the dangling C–F bonds, leading to the coexistence of crystallographic and amorphous domains. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> MXene terminal groups were reported to influence the cross-linking of pitch by consuming intrinsic hydrogen to achieve the transformation of the carbon structure from order to disorder. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> The introduction of oxygen functional groups triggers the cross-linking of pitch in the low-temperature preoxidation stage while preventing the fusion and ordered reconstruction of pitch in the high-temperature carbonization stage, thus generating a highly disordered structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> However, the introduction of oxygen-containing functional groups increases the diffusion energy of sodium ions at the interface and reduces electrical conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Using hard carbon precursors to consume excess hydrogen from soft carbon materials was reported to change the fusion state during pyrolysis and prevent microstructural ordering. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> These approaches mentioned above inhibit the graphitization by introducing a side chain or functional group, contributing to the capacity enhancement of carbon materials. Exploring different strategies to regulate the microcrystalline structure and inhibit graphitization is crucial for the development of low-cost yet high-performance carbon anodes for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we report a universal method of regulating the microcrystalline structure of polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon-derived carbon materials by various metal elements (including Mn, Nb, Ce, Cr, and V) for enhancing the specific capacity of carbon materials in SIBs. The microcrystalline structure was observed by structural characterization, while the chemical properties were investigated by multiple spectroscopies. The mechanism of Mn inhibiting graphitization was uncovered by real-time monitoring the evolution of the Mn element during thermal treatment using <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD), X-ray absorption near-edge structure, and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The battery performances of carbon materials were systematically evaluated. Finally, through correlating factors, including the specific capacity index, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value in Raman spectra, and theoretical calculation, we identify the “binding energy” as the descriptor to quantify the capability of metal elements in regulating the microcrystalline structure.</div></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">PAHC prepared from the direct carbonization of pitch without the Mn regulating the microcrystalline structure exhibits a blocky morphology with a size of 10–20 μm, as shown by the scanning electron microscope images (SEM, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>), which is attributed to the fusion and polymerization of pitch during the thermal treatment process. TEM and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of PAHC reveal that PAHC possesses a high degree of graphitization, as reflected by the highly ordered graphite microcrystalline domains with an interlayer spacing of 0.334 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b and c) and the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED, inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c) image. In contrast, the PAHC sample with Mn suppressing graphitization (PAHC-Mn) shows a lamellar morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d and e), which facilitates the material–electrolyte contact and reinforces the mass transfer of sodium ions. HRTEM of PAHC-Mn shows the coexistence of amorphous regions and graphite microcrystalline regions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f), which is apparently different from the highly graphitic property of PAHC. Moreover, PAHC-Mn exhibits an interlayer spacing of 0.393 nm, much larger than that of PAHC (0.334 nm), facilitating the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> mass transfer. The SAED image of PAHC-Mn indicates the apparent suppression of graphitization by the Mn element compared to PAHC (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f). HRTEM images and the corresponding elemental mapping image confirm the removal of the Mn element and the maintaining of morphology after acid washing (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2 and S3</a>). The XRD data reveal that the (002) peak of PAHC-Mn shows a shift toward a small angle compared to that of PAHC, indicating  the expansion of interlayer spacing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g), which is consistent with the HRTEM observation. The Raman spectrum of PAHC-Mn shows a disorder-induced D-band at 1360 cm<sup>–1</sup> and an in-plane vibrational G-band at 1585 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> It is noted that the D peak of PAHC-Mn exhibits a shift compared to that of PAHC. The D peak in the Raman spectra represents the degree of disorder/defect for carbon materials. PAHC-Mn with Mn suppressing the graphitization process has more amorphous phases than PAHC, thus exhibiting the phenomenon of the D peak shift. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> According to the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms curves, PAHC has almost no pore structure features, while PAHC-Mn shows a typical type IV isotherm with the presence of mesoporous structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> PAHC-Mn shows a higher specific surface area (114 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) than PAHC (40 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), which provides abundant storage sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, increases the mass transfer area at the material–electrolyte interface, and shortens the transport distance of Na<sup>+</sup> to improve the sodium storage performance.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/medium/nn3c08889_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Morphology and structure characterization of PAHC with or without Mn regulating the microcrystalline structure. (a) SEM image of PAHC. (b) TEM image of PAHC. (c) HRTEM image of PAHC. The inset shows the corresponding SAED image. (d) SEM image of PAHC-Mn. (e) TEM image of PAHC-Mn. (f) HRTEM image of PAHC-Mn. The inset shows the corresponding SAED image. (g) XRD of PAHC and PAHC-Mn. (h) Raman of PAHC and PAHC-Mn. (i) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of PAHC and PAHC-Mn.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c08889&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the mechanism for Mn regulating the microcrystalline structure of PAHC, we used <i>in situ</i> XRD to investigate the evolution of the Mn element during the thermal treatment process from 25 to 800 °C. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the (003) and (110) peaks of MnCl<sub>2</sub> (Mn precursor) are detected at 25 °C. During the temperature increase from 25 to 600 °C, these two peaks do not change significantly. However, from 600 °C onward, the (003) and (110) peaks of MnCl<sub>2</sub> disappear, whereas the three peaks at 36°, 41°, and 58° appear, which correspond to the (301), (211), and (312) peaks of MnO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. MnO<sub>2</sub> is formed as a result of the reaction between MnCl<sub>2</sub> and the O-containing functional group in pitch. When increasing the temperature to 800 °C, four peaks at 43°, 57°, 61°, 64°, and 67° are detected, which are indexed to the (220), (303), (224), (314), and (402) peaks of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, respectively. The following temperature holding process at 800 °C witnesses the coexistence of MnO<sub>2</sub> and Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The formation of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is due to the conversion of MnO<sub>2</sub>. This occurs at a high temperature of 800 °C, under which the carbon thermal reduction can proceed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Meanwhile, a peak with a large half-peak breadth at about 25° is detected at 745 °C and becomes stronger at 800 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), indicating the transformation of the pitch into carbon material with larger interlayer spacing and lower graphitization degree than the typical graphitic carbon materials. The TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) shows the thin lamellar structure of PAHC/Mn (PAHC after Mn suppressing graphitization without acid washing), while the HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) observes the presence of MnO<sub><i>x</i></sub> (manganese oxides) at the amorphous domain, which isolates the adjacent graphitic domains. This directly demonstrates that the Mn element promotes the formation of the amorphous regions and thus regulates the microcrystalline structure of the generated carbon materials. According to the TEM characterization, the size of manganese oxides is about 5 nm. The size of the manganese oxide intermediate will affect the pore size of PAHC-X due to the template effect. In addition, the pore size is related to both the amorphous carbon and carbon microcrystalline domains, where different carbon units, e.g., carbon dots, carbon particles, and carbon chains, will create pores by the aggregation effect.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/medium/nn3c08889_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Evolution of the Mn element during the process of microcrystalline structure regulation. (a) Isoplot of the <i>in situ</i> XRD results of PAHC/Mn. (b) XRD results of PAHC/Mn at different temperatures. (c) TEM image of PAHC/Mn. (d) HRTEM image of PAHC/Mn.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c08889&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) was employed to study the molecular structure of pitch, which reveals the existence of C═O, N-containing six-membered heterocycles, sulfur-containing five-membered heterocycles, and phenolic hydroxyl groups (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). Based on this NMR data and the previous works, a model of a pitch molecule is proposed (the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra were used to analyze the Mn element in PAHC/Mn (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). By comparing with the standard sample curves of MnO, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, MnO<sub>2</sub>, and Mn, it is found that the overall valence state of the Mn element is in the range between +2 and +3, corresponding to the valence state of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was also used to study the chemical bonds in the PAHC, PAHC/Mn, and PAHC-Mn samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). The results show that −C–O–C– was produced in PAHC/Mn and PAHC-Mn but absent in PAHC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> which is thought to be caused by the breakage of the C═O bond during the carbonization followed by bonding with the adjacent carbon atoms. In addition, the observation of the Mn–O bond confirms that the Mn element exists in the form of MnO<sub><i>x</i></sub> rather than bonding with the carbon matrix. The presence of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> makes the electronic state on the surrounding carbon structure significantly different compared to that of PAHC, which is manifested by a huge difference in the relative strength of the C═C bond. When Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is removed, the degree of polarization decreases, leading to a change in the relative intensity. High-resolution Mn 2p spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d) reveals the existence of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (640.8 eV), Mn<sup>3+</sup> (642.1 eV), and Mn<sup>4+</sup> (643.6 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> The existence of high-energy satellite structures with an energy of ∼646 eV indicates the typical exchange interaction of the d5 configuration. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> High-resolution C 1s spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e) show that C═C (283.5 eV), C–C (284.4 eV), and C═O (289.1 eV) are present in all three samples. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> However, PAHC/Mn has a −C–O–C– structure, while PAHC mainly has a C–O structure. Moreover, the intensity of C═O in PAHC/Mn is significantly reduced compared to that of PAHC. These results imply that the usage of the Mn element promotes the breakage of the C═O bond, which enables the regulation of the carbon microcrystalline structure during the thermal treatment process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> The C═C bonds in PAHC are due to the polycondensation reaction between polycyclic aromatic molecules during the carbonization process, which results in a highly graphitized carbon layer structure. PAHC/Mn is regulated by Mn elements to form a C–O–C structure, which prevents the aggregation and growth of polycyclic aromatic molecules to form a large and well-organized carbon layer structure. The high ratio of C–C bonds means that PAHC/Mn exhibits the microcrystalline structure of crystalline carbon and amorphous carbon, which facilitates the reversible deinsertion/insertion of sodium ions. The O 1s spectrum of PAHC/Mn shows four characteristic peaks, including the lattice oxygen (O<sup>2–</sup>) combined with metal at 529.2 eV, the hydroxyl group (−OH) at 532.5 eV, the lower-coordinated oxygen defect site (produced by Mn reaction with epoxy groups) at 531.2 eV, and the C–O group at 533.2 eV(<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 58 and 59">(58,59)</a> The C–O bond is detected in the O 1s spectrum of PAHC/Mn, while C═O is identified in the O 1s spectrum of PAHC, further confirming that C═O is converted to −C–O–C–. When the Mn element is used to modulate the microcrystalline structure, the Mn element can react with the oxygen element from the oxygen-containing functional groups in pitch to form manganese oxides, resulting in a lower oxygen content of PAHC-Mn compared to PAHC. This can be quantified by the related XPS O 1s data as provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. To simply show the role of the Mn element in regulating the graphitization behavior of pitch, the molecule structure of pitch is simplified as model α in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g without considering the N- and S-containing fragments due to the little effect on the graphitization process. Based on the above analysis, the mechanism of Mn regulating the carbon microcrystalline structure is proposed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g, route 2): (1) the pitch molecule (α) first reacts with MnCl<sub>2</sub> to produce β through complexation (replacing the hydrogen of the phenolic hydroxyl group in pitch molecules and producing hydrogen chloride), followed by β reacting with α to form £, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> (2) MnO<sub>2</sub> is removed via the thermal triggered bond breakage to form γ as an active intermediate with positive charge, and (3) γ reacts with α to produce η through the S<sub>N</sub>2 nucleophilic substitution reaction (the positively charged γ attacks the negatively charged C═O on α), forming the C–O–C bond to bridge the adjacent aromatic units. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a> The formation of −C–O–C– acts as a steric hindrance to inhibit the polymerization of adjacent fragments and thus inhibit graphitization. In contrast, pitch molecules tend to form the graphitic carbon materials via the aromatic polycondensation reaction during the direct carbonization (route 1).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/medium/nn3c08889_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Mechanism study of microcrystalline structure regulation. (a) Solid NMR of pitch. (b) XANES of PAHC/Mn. (c) FTIR of PAHC, PAHC/Mn, and PAHC-Mn. (d) High-resolution XPS spectra for Mn 2p of PAHC/Mn. (e) High-resolution XPS spectra for C 1s of PAHC/Mn and PAHC. (f) High-resolution XPS spectra for O 1s of PAHC/Mn and PAHC. (g) Schematic illustration of the carbonization processes of pitch with or without Mn regulation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c08889&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The promotion of sodium-ion storage by the Mn-regulated microcrystalline structure was investigated by using PAHC-Mn as the anode material for SIBs. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows the first five charge/discharge curves of PAHC-Mn at the current density of 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3 V. The initial capacity decay is due to the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and the irreversible insertion of sodium ions into the defect sites of carbon, which can be avoided by presodiation. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the typical charge–discharge profiles of the PAHC at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of PAHC is calculated to be 57%, which is higher than that of PAHC-Mn. This is due to the fact that after manganese element modulation, the carbon material exhibits a fluffy lamellar morphology with a larger specific surface area, which causes the electrolyte decomposition to form the SEI during the first discharge, resulting in a lower ICE. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, the PAHC-Mn anode has a rate capability of 390, 305, 250, 230, 180, and 140 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. After a sharp decrease of the current density from 2 to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity is restored to 390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the good structural stability of PAHC-Mn. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b shows the charge–discharge curves at different current densities, which show the slope-type profiles. The cyclic stability and Coulombic efficiency of the PAHC-Mn anode were evaluated at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). They show a high capacity retention of up to 91.9% after 1000 cycles associated with a high Coulombic efficiency of approximately 100%, indicating the high reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> storage and release in the repeated charge/discharge process. The high specific capacity of PAHC-Mn in this work surpasses most reported carbon-based anodes, such as hard carbon, biomass-derived carbon, and porous carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of  PAHC-Mn are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, which shows two small reduction peaks at about 0.8 and 1.2 V in the first cycle, corresponding to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of SEI on the electrode surface, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e shows the series of CV curves obtained at 0.2 to 1.0 mV/s, which reveal the hump shape at the voltage range of &lt;1 V, agreeing well with the charge/discharge curves in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. This electrochemical behavior of carbon materials with a larger interlayer distance (&gt;0.40 nm) shows an “adsorption” storage mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref63" aria-label="reference 20 and 63">(20,63)</a> The current and scan rate of the CV curve follow the formula: <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup> (<i>i</i> is current, <i>v</i> is scan rate, <i>a</i> is a constant, and <i>b</i> is an adjustable parameter). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f, the <i>b</i> values are 0.89 and 0.95 for the anodic and cathodic peaks, respectively, indicating that PAHC-Mn accommodates Na<sup>+</sup> mainly via an adsorption behavior. The contribution rate of surface adsorption ion storage can be quantitatively analyzed according to the formula <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are appropriate values and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> correspond to the surface adsorption contribution and diffusion-controlled reaction contribution, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> When the scan rate is increased from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the proportion of surface adsorption contribution gradually increases from 65.6% to 82.3% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g). The highly disordered structure in PAHC-Mn enables the high proportion of adsorption contribution and the fast ion mass transfer kinetics. The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (D<sub>Na+</sub>) in PAHC-Mn was determined by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) as provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. From  electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), the ohmic impedance at the starting point of the semicircle indicates the internal impedance of the electrode material itself (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The radius of the semicircle indicates the charge transfer impedance, which is related to the electrode/electrolyte interface process. The EIS data show that PAHC-Mn has a lower electric conductivity than PAHC due to the lowered graphitization degree featured by the long-range disorder.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/medium/nn3c08889_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of PAHC-Mn as the anode for SIBs. (a) Rate performance of PAHC-Mn. (b) Charge/discharge curves of PAHC-Mn at different current densities. (c) Long-term cycling performance of PAHC-Mn at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison of specific capacity at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> between PAHC-Mn and previous carbon-based electrodes. (e) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV/s. (f) Linear fit between log (current) and log (scan rate) at cathodic and anodic peaks. (g) Capacity contribution ratio of the capacitive- and diffusion-controlled behaviors at various CV scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c08889&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A universal method of regulating the microcrystalline structure of carbon materials by various metal elements was developed, and a descriptor is proposed to characterize the capability through correlating battery capacity with <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> in Raman spectra and the theoretical calculation results. The battery performance test shows that PAHC-Mn delivered the highest specific capacity (390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). In addition, the capacity of carbon materials with the microcrystalline structure regulation by different metals was PAHC-Mn (390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), PAHC-V (203 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), PAHC-Nb (175 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), PAHC-Cr (130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), PAHC-Ce (123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), and PAHC (90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), which is consistent with the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value in the Raman spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b and c). This indicates that metal elements regulate the carbon microcrystalline structure and thus promote  Na<sup>+</sup> storage. The morphologies of  PAHC-M (M = Cr, Ce, V,  Nb) are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9–S12</a>. According to theoretical calculations, different metal elements have different binding energies for oxygen on the aromatic molecules of pitch, among which manganese has the highest binding energy for oxygen, which affects the aggregation and polymerization behavior of the polycyclic aromatic molecules, and thus the final carbon materials show different morphologies. Therefore, the introduction of different metals will lead to different morphologies. The XRD results of PAHC/M (M = Nb, Ce, Cr, or V) after the thermal treatment show that the metal elements exist in the form of metal oxides (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>d–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13</a>). This is attributed to the mutual principle that chlorine elements in these metal chlorides replace H elements of the phenolic hydroxyl group in pitch, forming a C–O–M structure, which then breaks and forms metal oxides, thus regulating the microcrystalline structure and inhibiting the graphitization. During this process, these metal elements show different binding energies and thus exhibit different capabilities for regulating the microcrystalline structure of carbon materials. The β structures in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g generated by different metal elements are provided in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g. The theoretical calculation shows that the structure generated using Mn is the most stable (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h), promoting the formation of η structures, which are conducive to inhibiting graphitization and regulating the microstructure. The index of the binding energy of different metal elements matches well with the data of both specific capacities and <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values in the Raman spectra. Hence, we identified the “binding energy” as the descriptor to characterize the capability of various metal chlorides in regulating the microcrystalline structure of carbon materials for improving the sodium-ion storage performance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/medium/nn3c08889_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Proposed descriptor for characterizing the capability of regulating the carbon microcrystalline structure by different metal elements. (a) Charge/discharge profiles of PAHC-M (M = Mn, Nb, Ce, Cr, or V) at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Raman spectra of PAHC and PAHC-M. (c) Relationship between the specific capacity and <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> (in Raman spectra) of PAHC-M. XRD patterns of (d) PAHC/Ce, (e) PAHC/Cr, and (f) PAHC/V. (g) Structures of PAHC and different metal–PAHC composites after  structure optimization. (h) Binding energy of PAHC/M (M = Mn, Nb, Ce, Cr, or V).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/asset/images/large/nn3c08889_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c08889&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we reported a universal metal-assisted regulation of carbon microcrystalline structure, which provides a special way for improving the sodium storage performance of carbon materials. The results show that Mn could effectively regulate the graphite microcrystalline structure based on the formation of the C–O–C, which serves as the steric hindrance between the adjacent aromatic fragments. Furthermore, our research demonstrates that the carbon material has a super high capacity of 390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, outperforming most of the carbon anodes of sodium-ion batteries reported previously. The specific capacities obtained by using different metals to control the graphite microcrystalline structure are consistent with the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios and the theoretical calculation results. This identifies the binding energy between metal chlorides and PAHC as the descriptor for quantifying the capability of metal regulating the microcrystalline structure of carbon materials. These findings provide valuable guidance for improving the performance of pitch-based carbon materials by understanding the aggregation evolution mechanism of microscopic aromatic carbon fragments.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Synthesis of PAHC/Mn</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The active material is prepared by the solid-phase thermal treatment method. Specifically, 100 mg of pitch was dispersed in 50 mL of ethanol, followed by adding 300 mg of manganese chloride (MnCl<sub>2</sub>). After stirring for 3 h, the sample was dried in an oven at 85 °C for 5 h. Then, it was calcined at 800 °C for 2 h in a tubular furnace under a nitrogen atmosphere with a heating rate of 8 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. After cooling to room temperature, the black powder (PAHC/Mn) was collected.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of PAHC-Mn</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Concentrated hydrochloric acid was diluted with  deionized water in a ratio of 1:1 by volume as the pickling agent. Then, PAHC/Mn was put into the pickling agent and sonicated for 5 min, followed by water washing to the state of pH = 7. Next, the black powder was collected and dried in an oven at 85 °C for 24 h, obtaining a sample of PAHC-Mn.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Synthesis of PAHC-M (M = Ce, Cr, V, Nb)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The previous experimental steps were repeated by replacing MnCl<sub>2</sub> with MCl<sub><i>x</i></sub> (cerium chloride, vanadium chloride, chromium chloride, and niobium chloride) while keeping the same carbonization temperature, holding time, heating rate, N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, and mass ratio of pitch to MCl<sub><i>x</i></sub> (1:3).</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Synthesis of PAHC</h3><div class="NLM_p last">PAHC was obtained by directly carbonizing pitch at 800 °C for 2 h under a nitrogen atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies of PAHC, PAHC/M, and PAHC-M were observed using a transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEOL, 4000EX). The crystalline structures of these samples were characterized using X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku, TTR-III) with  Cu Kα radiation. The chemical states of the sample on the surface were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), Raman microscopy (633 nm laser excitation, Thermo Fisher Scientific DXR), and Fourier transform infrared (FTIR, Bruker Equinox 55). The ion chromatography analysis was performed using a Thermo Fisher ICS-5000. The X-ray absorption fine structure spectra data (Mn K-edge) were collected at the 1W1B station in Beijing Synchrotron Radiation Facility (BSRF, operated at 2.5 GeV with a maximum current of 250 mA).</div></div><div id="sec3_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Test</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performances were tested using the CR2032 coin type half-cells at  room temperature. The coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.1 ppm). Working electrodes were prepared by mixing active materials, Super P, and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose at a mass ratio of 8:1:1. Then, ultrapure water was used as the solvent to prepare a slurry. The slurry was coated on Al foil and dried at 100 °C for 12 h under vacuum. A sodium foil was used as the counter electrode, and glass fiber was used as the separator. 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DEC (v/v 1:1) with 5% FEC was used as the electrolyte. The galvanostatic charge/discharge test was conducted using a Landt CT3002A battery test system with a voltage window of 0.01–3.00 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted using the IviumStat electrochemical workstation.</div></div><div id="sec3_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Density Functional Theory Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All simulations reported in this work were performed within the framework of DFT with the B3LYP hybrid functional, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> which was implemented in the CP2K/Quickstep code. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The core electrons were described by Goedecker–Teter–Hutter (GTH) pseudopotentials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> and the valence electrons were described by a mixed Gaussian and plane waves basis (GPW). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> The wave functions were expanded on a double-ζ valence polarized (DZVP) basis set along with an auxiliary plane wave basis set at a cutoff energy of 500 Ry. And the exchange and correlation potentials are approximated using the Brillouin zone by the gamma approximation. The convergence criterion for energy was set to 10<sup>–12</sup> Hartree, and that for the self-consistent field was 10<sup>–6</sup> Hartree.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional information for the characterization of samples and the electrochemical data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c08889/suppl_file/nn3c08889_si_001.pdf">nn3c08889_si_001.pdf (874.32 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.3c08889" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>51</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">4484</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4536</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ether-based electrolytes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Ying; Wu, Feng; Li, Yu; Liu, Mingquan; Feng, Xin; Bai, Ying; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">51</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4484-4536</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be strong candidates for large-scale energy storage with the benefits of cost-effectiveness and sodium abundance.  Reliable electrolytes, as ionic conductors that regulate the electrochem. reaction behavior and the nature of the interface and electrode, are indispensable in the development of advanced SIBs with high Coulombic efficiency, stable cycling performance and high rate capability.  Conventional carbonate-based electrolytes encounter numerous obstacles for their wide application in SIBs due to the formation of a dissolvable, continuous-thickening solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer and inferior stability with electrodes.  Comparatively, ether-based electrolytes (EBEs) are emerging in the secondary battery field with fascinating properties to improve the performance of batteries, esp. SIBs.  Their stable solvation structure enables highly reversible solvent-co-intercalation reactions and the formation of a thin and stable SEI.  However, although EBEs can provide more stable cycling and rapid sodiation kinetics in electrodes, benefitting from their favorable electrolyte/electrode interactions such as chem. compatibility and good wettability, their special chem. is still being investigated and puzzling.  In this review, we provide a thorough and comprehensive overview on the developmental history, fundamental characteristics, superiorities and mechanisms of EBEs, together with their advances in other battery systems.  Notably, the relation among electrolyte science, interfacial chem. and electrochem. performance is highlighted, which is of great significance for the in-depth understanding of battery chem.  Finally, future perspectives and potential directions are proposed to navigate the design and optimization of electrolytes and electrolyte/electrode interfaces for advanced batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE02074C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kopold, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van Aken, P.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uniform yolk-shell Sn4P3@C nanospheres as high-capacity and cycle-stable anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Kopold, Peter; Wu, Chao; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Uniform yolk-shell Sn4P3@C nanospheres were facilely synthesized via a top-down phosphorized route with yolk-shell Sn@C nanospheres as the precursor.  As anode materials for Na-ion batteries, they exhibit very high reversible capacity (790 mA h g-1), superior rate capability (reversible capabilities of 720, 651, 581, 505, and 421 mA h g-1 at 0.2C, 0.4C, 0.8C, 1.5C, and 3C, resp.) and stable cycling performance (a high capacity of 360 mA h g-1 at 1.5C after long 400 cycles).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201901351"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Extended “Adsorption-Insertion” Model: A New Insight into the Sodium Storage Mechanism of Hard Carbons</span>. <i>Adv.                 10.1002/aenm.201901351</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D9%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1901351%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DN.%2BSun%26author%3DZ.%2BGuan%26author%3DY.%2BLiu%26author%3DY.%2BCao%26author%3DQ.%2BZhu%26author%3DH.%2BLiu%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DP.%2BZhang%26author%3DB.%2BXu%26title%3DExtended%2B%25E2%2580%259CAdsorption-Insertion%25E2%2580%259D%2BModel%253A%2BA%2BNew%2BInsight%2Binto%2Bthe%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BMechanism%2Bof%2BHard%2BCarbons%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201901351&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.3c08889&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201901351&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling Fast Na+ Transfer Kinetics in the Whole-Voltage-Region of Hard-Carbon Anodes for Ultrahigh-Rate Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Xiuping; Lu, Zhixiu; Wang, Jing; Feng, Xiaochen; Roy, Swagata; Liu, Xiangsi; Yang, Yong; Zhao, Yufeng; Zhang, Jiujun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">34</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2109282</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficient electrode materials, that combine high power and high energy, are the crucial requisites of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which have unwrapped new possibilities in the areas of grid-scale energy storage.  Hard carbons (HCs) are considered as the leading candidate anode materials for SIBs, however, the primary challenge of slow charge-transfer kinetics at the low potential region (&lt;0.1 V) remains unresolved till date, and the underlying structure-performance correlation is under debate.  Herein, ultrafast sodium storage in the whole-voltage-region (0.01-2 V), with the Na+ diffusion coeff. enhanced by 2 orders of magnitude (≈10-7 cm2 s-1) through rationally deploying the phys. parameters of HCs using a ZnO-assisted bulk etching strategy is reported.  It is unveiled that the Na+ adsorption energy (Ea) and diffusion barrier (Eb) are in a pos. and neg. linear relationship with the carbon p-band center, resp., and balance of Ea and Eb is crit. in enhancing the charge-storage kinetics.  The charge-storage mechanism in HCs is evidenced through comprehensive in(ex) situ techniques.  The as prepd. HCs microspheres deliver a record high rate performance of 107 mAh g-1 @ 50 A g-1 and unprecedented electrochem. performance at extremely low temp. (426 mAh g-1 @ -40°C).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202212186"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jia, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.-H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reconfiguring Hard Carbons with Emerging Sodium-Ion Batteries: A Perspective</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>35</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2212186</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reconfiguring Hard Carbons with Emerging Sodium-Ion Batteries: A Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chu, Yue; Zhang, Jun; Zhang, Yibo; Li, Qi; Jia, Yiran; Dong, Ximan; Xiao, Jing; Tao, Ying; Yang, Quan-Hong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">35</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">31</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2212186</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbons, an important category of amorphous carbons, are non-graphitizable and are widely accepted as the most promising anode materials for emerging sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), because of their changeable low-potential charge/discharge plateaus.  However, their microstructures are not fixed and are difficult to accurately demonstrate as graphites do.  The successful use of hard carbons in SIBs revives the interest to clearly picture their complicated microstructures that are in close relevance to sodium storage.  In this review, the past definitions and structural models of hard carbons are revisited first, and a renewed understanding of their sodium storage is presented.  Three crit. structural features are highlighted for hard carbons, namely crystallites, defects, and nanopores, which are directly responsible for the presence of the low-potential plateaus and their reversible extension.  The impact of these structural features upon the sodium storage is then deeply discussed and sieving carbons is finally proposed as an ideal configuration of carbon anode for superhigh sodium storage.  This review is expected to offer a clear picture of hard carbons, and help realize a truly rational design of high-capacity carbon anodes, driving the industrialization of SIBs, and more promisingly open up a window for exploring their possible new uses.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2023.01.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Soomro, R.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-017-1537-z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FeSe<sub>2</sub> Clusters with Excellent Cyclability and Rate Capability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3202</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3211</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">FeSe2 clusters with excellent cyclability and rate capability for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Xiujuan; Tang, Chunjuan; An, Qinyou; Yan, Mengyu; Wang, Xuanpeng; Hu, Ping; Cai, Xinyin; Mai, Liqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3202-3211</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRAEB5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have great promise for sustainable and economical energy-storage applications.  Nevertheless, it is a major challenge to develop anode materials with high capacity, high rate capability, and excellent cycling stability for them.  In this study, FeSe2 clusters consisting of nanorods were synthesized by a facile hydrothermal method, and their sodium-storage properties were investigated with different electrolytes.  The FeSe2 clusters delivered high electrochem. performance with an ether-based electrolyte in a voltage range of 0.5-2.9 V.  A high discharge capacity of 515 mAh·g-1 was obtained after 400 cycles at 1 A·g-1, with a high initial columbic efficiency of 97.4%.  Even at an ultrahigh rate of 35 A·g-1, a specific capacity of 128 mAh·g-1 was achieved.  Using calcns., we revealed that the pseudocapacitance significantly contributed to the sodium-ion storage, esp. at high current rates, leading to a high rate capability.  The high comprehensive performance of the FeSe2 clusters makes them a promising anode material for SIBs.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cellular carbon-wrapped FeSe2 nanocavities with ultrathin walls and multiple rooms for ion diffusion-confined ultrafast sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tang, Yongchao; Zhao, Zongbin; Hao, Xiaojuan; Wei, Yue; Zhang, Han; Dong, Yanfeng; Wang, Yuwei; Pan, Xin; Hou, Yanan; Wang, Xuzhen; Qiu, Jieshan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4469-4479</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with high energy/power d. have been in urgent demand for various power applications.  Upgrading the electrochem. dynamics of electrode materials is crucial to achieve this goal.  Herein, we exhibit delicately synthesized cellular carbon-wrapped FeSe2 nanocavities (h-FeSe2@PC) with sub-5 nm ultrathin walls and multiple rooms for ultrafast sodium storage.  Unlike with the conventional interphase Kirkendall effect, the multi-room FeSe2 nanocavities were formed via an exotic stress-induced cracking-hollowing reassembly mechanism.  Benefiting from their unique multi-hierarchy, more than 93% pseudocapacitive capacity contribution was recognized in an h-FeSe2@PC anode, featuring ultrafast dynamics.  As a result, h-FeSe2@PC displayed an extremely low-decay (9.5%) rate capability, ultrastable cyclability (over 2000 loops with 98.5% capacity retention), and high energy/power densities (670.7 W h kg-1/35.3 kW kg-1 at 20 A g-1).  The results of multiple analyses and comparisons showed that the dynamics-promoted superior performance was correlated almost completely to the applied FeSe2 in h-FeSe2@PC by shortening the Na+-diffusion length to near the accessible limit (&lt;10 nm) and to the fast electron transfer ensured by the cellular carbon.  The exhibited dynamics-oriented materials design could open up an avenue to aid the further development of high-performance electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700622"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Homogenous metallic deposition regulated by defect-rich skeletons for sodium metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Zhen; Guo, Zhenyu; Madhu, Rajesh; Xie, Fei; Chen, Ruixuan; Wang, Jing; Tebyetekerwa, Mike; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6381-6393</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal batteries are attracting increasing attention on account of their high energy densities as well as the abundance of sodium-based resources.  However, the uneven metallic deposition and dendrite formation during cycling hinder the application of sodium metal anodes.  Carbon skeletons have been reported in the literature to mitigate the dendrite formation during the plating and stripping of metallic sodium.  Still, the role played by different carbon structural features (i.e., pores vs. defects) and relative mechanisms are not well understood, preventing the controllable interface engineering at the anode side.  Here, we have rationally designed the structural features of sustainable carbon skeletons from a renewable precursor to unveil the roles of defects and pores for metallic deposition.  The obtained carbon skeleton with rich defects and negligible pores exhibits the best performance when applied to protect metal anodes.  After long cycling (&gt;1200 h), the retained high Coulombic efficiency (~ 99.9%) of the plating and stripping processes indicates the importance of defects for inducing uniform metallic deposition.  Combined with different types of cathodes (e.g., Prussian blue and sulfur), "anode-less" sodium metal batteries with enhanced electrochem. performance are also demonstrated in terms of sustainability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201804458"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanostructured Conversion-Type Negative Electrode Materials for Low-Cost and High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.
